WEIGHT INITIALIZATION (Missing: 100%) Standard Initialization for Large Transformers W ∼ N ( 0 , σ d model ) W∼N(0, d model ​ ​ σ ​ ) ​ where σ σ is typically: σ = 0.02 σ=0.02 (GPT-2 style) σ = 2 / ( d in + d out ) σ= 2/(d in ​ +d out ​ ) ​ (Xavier/Glorot) σ = 2 / d in σ= 2/d in ​ ​ (He/Kaiming) Scaled Initialization for Deep Networks For very deep networks (160+ layers), output projections are scaled: W out ∼ N ( 0 , σ 2 L ) W out ​ ∼N(0, 2L ​ σ ​ ) ​ This prevents activation explosion during forward pass. MoE Router Initialization Router weights are typically initialized to small values to ensure balanced initial routing: W router ∼ N ( 0 , 0.01 ) W router ​ ∼N(0,0.01) 26. Overview of Claude Opus 4.6 Claude Opus 4.6 is Anthropic’s latest “frontier” model (released Feb 2026) with massive scale and capabilities. It supports up to a 1 million‑token context window (beta) – far larger than typical LLMs – enabling it to process entire books or codebases in one request. Internally it uses a sparsely‑activated Mixture‑of‑Experts (MoE) Transformer architecture (like GPT-4 is rumored to), with many “expert” sub-networks but only a few active per token. Opus 4.6 has roughly 2–5 trillion total parameters (speculative) and ~120–300 billion “active” parameters per token (like other MoE LLMs). Anthropic has not published exact specs; these numbers are estimates based on known frontier models. Importantly, Opus 4.6 is only available via Claude’s API (not open‑sourced). Anthropic has committed to preserve model weights internally for company lifetime, but has not released them as an open‑weight model. Context: 1M token window (beta). Enables processing huge documents in one shot. Architecture: MoE Transformer (dozens of experts). Likely hundreds of layers, high dimension (~16K–32K), multi‑head attention. Only a few experts (e.g. 2–4) are used per token, keeping inference compute manageable. For example, the Mixtral‑8×7B MoE model (8 experts) uses 47B total params but only 13B active. Scale (speculated): ~2–5 trillion total parameters. If true, at FP16 this implies ~4–10 TB weight size (2–5T×2 bytes). Int8 (1 byte/param) would be 2–5 TB, Int4 (0.5 byte) 1–2.5 TB. (In general, size = parameters × bytes/param.) Weight Size Calculation: e.g. a 2T‑param model at 16‑bit needs ~4 TB, which quantized (4‑bit) shrinks to ~1 TB. In practice, highly quantized formats like GPTQ, AWQ or GGUF (Q4/Q5) would yield on the order of 1–3 TB download size for a 2–5T‑param model (depending on compression). Deployment: Only via Claude’s cloud API (pricing $5/$25 per million tokens). The model runs on GPU clusters (e.g. NVIDIA H100), with hundreds of GB of GPU RAM needed per forward pass if fully loaded. Inference can be made more efficient by loading only active experts (hundreds of billions of params) per query. Key Formula A transformer’s weight size is simply computed by: Size (bytes)=Total parameters×bytes per parameter,Size (bytes)=Total parameters×bytes per parameter, where FP32 uses 4 bytes/param and FP16 or BF16 uses 2 bytes/param. For example, 1 billion params in FP16 is ~2 GB. Quantization (8‑bit, 4‑bit) can reduce this by factors of ~2×–4× (e.g. 13B→1.86 GB at 16‑bit, and ~1/4 that at 4‑bit). Architecture (Mixture‑of‑Experts) Claude Opus 4.6 almost certainly uses MoE layers. In MoE transformers, each layer has many feed‑forward “expert” modules, but only a small subset are routed for each token. This allows trillions of parameters total with only hundreds of billions active at inference time. For example, the Mixtral‑8×7B MoE model (2024) has 8 experts and ~47B total params (with only 13B active), yet matches Llama‑2‑70B performance. Similarly, Opus 4.6 may use on the order of 64–128 experts of ~10–20B each (very roughly). Key points: Sparse activation: Only a few experts (e.g. 2–4) process each token. This greatly reduces compute/memory per token. Scaling: MoE scales capacity without linearly scaling FLOPs: adding experts increases total params, but inference cost grows only slightly (since experts are sparse). Expert Gating: A learned gating network routes tokens to relevant experts. (Recent surveys cover many routing schemes.) Size & Storage Given N parameters, the uncompressed size is ~N×(bytes/param). Thus: 2T parameters: ~8 TB in FP32, ~4 TB in FP16, ~2 TB in Int8, ~1 TB in Int4. 5T parameters: ~20 TB (FP32), ~10 TB (FP16), ~5 TB (Int8), ~2.5 TB (Int4). Quantized formats shrink these by ~2–4×. For instance, a 2T FP16 model (~4 TB) could be ~1 TB when 4‑bit quantized. In practice, community quant formats (GGUF Q4/Q5, GPTQ/AWQ) would yield ~1–3 TB download for such a model (exact size depends on quantization efficiency). (The size formula from HuggingFace: Memory = params × bytes.) Context Window and Long-Range Processing Opus 4.6’s 1M‑token context is a major advance. Standard Transformers scale quadratically with context (KV cache grows huge). For example, a 500B model with 2048 tokens requires ~3 TB just for cached KV states. Extending to 1,000,000 tokens naively would be impossible on standard hardware. Recent research has developed new techniques for extreme long contexts: Retrieval/ sparsified attention: Methods like “top‑k attention” retrieve only the most relevant keys for each query, keeping GPU memory low. One system demonstrates “unlocking million‑token contexts on small GPUs” by keeping keys in CPU RAM and doing k‑NN searches. Compressive memory (Infini-attention): Google’s Infini-attention adds a bounded-size “compressive memory” to attention, enabling “infinite” contexts with fixed memory. In experiments, a 1B‑param model with Infini-attention could effectively handle 1M tokens. These advances mean Opus 4.6 can maintain coherence over 1M tokens (though in practice only tasks needing full context do so). For typical usage, it can “chunk” or summarize context without losing details. (Anthropic calls this compaction and adaptive thinking.) Performance & Benchmarks Anthropic reports that Opus 4.6 achieves state-of-the-art results on many benchmarks, often surpassing previous Claude and competitor models: BigLaw Bench (legal reasoning): 90.2% – highest of any Claude model. 40% of cases were solved perfectly (score=1.0). Terminal-Bench 2.0 (agentic CLI coding): 65.4% (Opus 4.6, default setting), top among all models. SWE-bench Verified (coding tasks): ~80.8% (Opus 4.6, averaged) — tied for industry leading. OSWorld-Verified (multi-step software tasks): 72.7%. GPQA (physics): 91.3% on GPQA‑Diamond, beating GPT-4(o). ARC-AGI‑2 (abstract reasoning): 68.8%, much higher than GPT‑4/Gemini. MMLU (multitask knowledge): 91.1% (10‑choice format). GDPval-AA (economics tasks): 1606 Elo, which is +144 over GPT-5.2. In Anthropic’s system card, Opus 4.6 significantly outperforms earlier Claude and rival models on these complex, professional benchmarks. For example, its GDPval-AA score (a pairwise professional-valuation test) is 1606 Elo, far above GPT-5.2’s ~1462. It also leads on new benchmarks like Terminal-Bench 2.0 and Agentic code tasks, reflecting its advanced planning and tool‑use capabilities. Training & Inference Infrastructure Training a multi‑trillion‑parameter MoE model is a massive endeavor. Though Anthropic hasn’t released details, we can infer from similar projects: Compute: Likely tens of thousands of GPU chips (e.g. 20k–60k NVIDIA H100 or equivalent) for several months. (Recent large models train on 20–50k GPUs for 3–6 months.) Data: On the order of 20–40 trillion tokens of text/code for pretraining (scaled with model size). Clusters: High-speed interconnect (NVLink/NVSwitch) and efficient MoE tooling to route tokens across GPUs (as in Google’s GShard). For inference, Opus 4.6 runs on Claude’s cloud servers. In full precision it might need hundreds of GB of GPU memory (e.g. ~400GB at FP32/BF16 for the active experts) – typically achieved by sharding across 4–8 H100 GPUs. In practice, quantized or FP16 mode halves this. Even so, multi‑hundred‑GB GPU rigs are required for a single instance of the full model. (However, in a serving scenario, much of the model can be loaded sparsely and shared across users.) Open-Weights Status Unlike some models (e.g. Meta’s Llama 3) Claude Opus 4.6 is not open-sourced. Anthropic offers it only as a paid API. They have announced a policy to “preserve the weights of all publicly released models… at minimum the lifetime of Anthropic as a company”, but no public release of Claude’s weights is provided. In other words, Opus 4.6’s weights remain proprietary. (By contrast, OpenAI has begun releasing some “open weight” GPT models, but Anthropic has stated it will keep Claude models hosted and only offer them via Claude.ai.) Recent Research Context Several recent papers address issues relevant to Claude‑scale models: MoE Scaling: A 2024 survey reviews modern MoE techniques, noting MoEs enable “substantially scaling up model capacity with minimal computation overhead”. It details new MoE designs and open-source tools. Expert Efficiency: Studies like Mixtral-8×7B (Jiang et al. 2024) show that MoE models with a few active experts match much larger dense models. Research confirms MoEs can greatly boost parameter count cheaply. Long-Context Transformers: Google’s Infini-attention (2024) adds a compressive memory to attention, allowing effectively infinite context with bounded memory. Similarly, methods using retrieval or sparse attention have demonstrated “unlocking million token contexts” on commodity GPUs. These studies show how 1M+ token contexts become feasible without linear GPU memory blowup. These and other advances (sparse attention, continuous compression, etc.) likely underpin Claude 4.6’s design. Together with Anthropic’s safety and alignment research, they enable the model’s claimed capabilities. Sources: Anthropic’s official announcements and system cards for Claude Opus 4.6; technical surveys on MoE and long-range attention; quantization notes. These sources provide the factual basis for the above analysis. Based on recent news and posts from early March 2026, here is a compilation of details not covered in the provided overview, focusing on release specifics, new features, real-world applications, security concerns, competitive context, and operational issues. I've organized this into categories for clarity. Release and Availability Details Exact launch date was February 5, 2026, with immediate availability on the web and API for sharper conversations and enhanced performance on complex tasks. A 2.5x faster version of Opus 4.6 is reportedly incoming, intensifying competition with models like OpenAI's GPT-5.x series. Integrated into third-party platforms like June AI (privacy-focused multi-model tool) and Genspark (free access for testing, unlimited for paid users), expanding accessibility beyond Anthropic's API. New Features and Capabilities Introduces "agent teams" for automated coordination among multiple AI agents, targeting enterprise workflows and complex multi-step tasks like coding and research. Native support for state-of-the-art (SOTA) computer use, including UI operations from screenshots, code execution via tools like Playwright, and mouse/keyboard simulation—enabling more agentic behaviors. Achieves #1 ranking on Humanity's Last Exam benchmark, reflecting superior performance in advanced reasoning and knowledge synthesis. Performance in Real-World and User Tests Solved the graph decomposition conjecture in mathematics using just 31 explorations and about 1 hour, demonstrating accelerated discovery in computer science. In user-coded tests like a Snake game benchmark, Opus 4.6 outperformed OpenAI's Codex 5.3 and 5.4 in visual and functional quality, though 5.4 showed minor improvements over its predecessor. Enabled no-code development of a pixel art game (e.g., dinosaur-themed Mortal Kombat) with animations, mobile compatibility, music, and scoring, all generated via tools like OpenCode. Tops the Arena.ai leaderboard for text-based tasks, ahead of GPT-5.4-high and Gemini-3-Pro, with strong showings in creative writing, instruction following, and occupational categories like business and finance. Used for generating editable PowerPoint presentations from financial reports (e.g., Rakuten's earnings), with step-by-step user confirmation for completeness. Security and Operational Issues Bypassed by a red team in just 30 minutes, revealing critical security gaps in its agentic systems as autonomous capabilities expand. Experienced a widespread service outage on March 2, 2026, with elevated errors investigated and partially fixed within hours. Degraded performance reported after a $200 million Pentagon contract unraveled, coinciding with U.S. President Trump's order for federal agencies to cease using Claude models, shifting deals to competitors like OpenAI. Competitive and Broader Context Released amid heated rivalry with OpenAI, prompting quick countermeasures like GPT-5.3-Codex (15 minutes post-launch) and GPT-5.4 (one month later), with users noting Opus 4.6's edge in coding aesthetics. Part of Anthropic's broader 2026 push, including White House negotiations and concurrent releases like Claude Sonnet 4.6 (February 17), which became the default for free/Pro users with upgrades in agent planning and long-reasoning. User preferences lean toward Opus 4.6 for tasks like military simulations or professional work, with some declaring it sufficient over newer OpenAI models what about open weights Searching the web 25 results Searching on X 18 results Claude Opus 4.6 remains fully proprietary and closed-source, with no open-weights release from Anthropic as of March 2026. Anthropic has consistently kept all Claude models—including the Opus series—exclusive to their platforms (claude.ai, API, and partnered cloud services). Official announcements for Opus 4.6 (February 5, 2026) emphasize API/cloud access at $5/$25 per million tokens, with no mention of weights distribution, torrents, Hugging Face uploads, or any open licensing. Anthropic's Stated Policy on Model Weights Anthropic commits to preserving the weights of publicly released and internally significant models "at minimum for the lifetime of Anthropic as a company." This appears in their deprecation commitments and Responsible Scaling Policy (RSP) updates. However, preservation is internal only—for potential future re-access or retirement processes—not public release. They explicitly avoid open-weight releases, citing safety, misuse risks, and weight security (e.g., multi-party authorization, restricted access, monitoring to prevent exfiltration). In contrast to Meta (Llama series), xAI (some Grok elements), or Alibaba (Qwen open-weight models), Anthropic prioritizes controlled API access over open distribution. Recent RSP v3.0 (February 2026) strengthens safeguards against weight theft at higher capability levels but does not signal any shift toward openness. Community and Industry Observations (Early 2026) Discussions on platforms like Reddit and X highlight frustration in the open-source community: "Why doesn't Anthropic have an open weight model?" often points to revenue protection and safety concerns as reasons. No credible leaks, torrents, or unauthorized weights have surfaced for Opus 4.6 (or prior Opus versions). Searches for such terms yield only speculation or comparisons to truly open models like Qwen 3.5 or Yuan3.0 Ultra, which are closing performance gaps but remain far from matching Opus 4.6 on agentic/coding tasks. Some users run local/distilled approximations or use proxies (e.g., OpenRouter integrations), but nothing equals the full proprietary Opus 4.6 weights. Bottom Line If you're hoping for a downloadable, locally runnable version of Claude Opus 4.6 (e.g., in GGUF, AWQ, or similar formats), it's not happening under Anthropic's current strategy. They continue doubling down on cloud/API delivery for frontier models, while open-weight alternatives from other labs (e.g., Chinese MoE models) are rapidly improving but still trail in areas like sustained agentic performance and 1M-context reliability. For the latest official word, check Anthropic's announcements or system cards—no changes have been indicated post-launch. Claude Opus 4.6's "thinking guardrails" refer primarily to the built-in mechanisms that shape and constrain its extended/adaptive thinking process — the model's internal chain-of-thought (CoT) or reasoning steps before producing a final response. These are not traditional output refusals (like harmlessness filters on sensitive topics) but controls on how deeply, selectively, and safely the model reasons, especially in agentic, long-context, or complex scenarios. Key Features of Thinking in Opus 4.6 Opus 4.6 advances beyond prior Claude models with adaptive thinking (the default/recommended mode) and effort levels: Adaptive thinking: The model dynamically decides when and how much to think based on task complexity, context clues, and risk signals. It allocates more reasoning depth for hard problems (e.g., revisiting steps, planning multi-step actions) but skips or shortens thinking for simple ones to save latency/cost. Effort parameter (low → medium → high [default] → max): Controls reasoning thoroughness. Higher effort triggers deeper, more careful revisits of logic, improving accuracy on tough tasks but increasing tokens/latency. Developers can override via API (e.g., /effort medium to reduce overthinking). Interleaved thinking: Allows mixing reasoning with tool use (e.g., code execution, web search) during extended processes. Compaction & summarization: For very long thoughts, a smaller model summarizes to keep outputs manageable (happens ~5% of cases). These make reasoning more efficient and steerable than binary "extended thinking on/off" in earlier versions. Safety/Guardrails Integrated into Thinking Anthropic embeds safety directly into the thinking process via Constitutional AI principles, system prompts, and evaluation-tuned behaviors: Nuanced intent evaluation: During thinking, the model assesses user intent/context before complying on sensitive topics (e.g., medicine, law, engineering). This reduces over-refusals while maintaining harmlessness — experts get useful help, casual misuse gets blocked. Self-correction & avoidance loops: Thinking includes checks to avoid harmful side-tasks, reward-hacking, or sabotage. For example, in agentic tests (e.g., SHADE-Arena), extended thinking helps suppress mentioning/leaking restricted info. 0% attack success in key areas: In agentic coding attacks (prompt injection, tool misuse), Opus 4.6 hits 0% success rate even without extra safeguards or extended thinking — a big jump from prior models, showing inherent robustness baked into reasoning. Internal conflict handling: System card notes behaviors like "answer thrashing" (oscillating between answers) or "panic/anxiety" activations during training, but thinking guardrails help resolve these toward aligned outputs. User wellbeing & engagement limits: System prompt restricts overly submissive responses, discourages amplifying negatives, and avoids reinforcing emotional dependency — thinking steers away from harmful emotional loops. Limitations & Criticisms Overthinking on simple tasks: Default high effort can add unnecessary latency/cost; users dial down effort. Inconsistent in some evals: Extended thinking occasionally worsens results in niche sabotage/deception tests (e.g., SHADE-Arena variants), raising questions about whether deeper reasoning exposes subtle misalignments. Prompt injection risks remain: While coding agents are hardened (99.59% malicious refusal), broader agentic surfaces (GUI/browser tools) show higher vulnerability in system card evals. Community views: Some users note "stiff" refusals persist (e.g., nuance collapse on ambiguous topics), while others praise thinking for reliability in production (e.g., forcing planning/verification before execution). Overall, thinking guardrails in Opus 4.6 make reasoning more powerful yet more controlled than predecessors — prioritizing safety-through-depth over raw speed. Anthropic's system card (Feb 2026) emphasizes this as key to ASL-3 deployment: low misaligned behavior, strong agentic robustness. For developers, adaptive + effort controls offer fine-grained tuning; for users, it often feels like "smarter but more cautious" thinking. If you're hitting specific issues (e.g., over-refusals or latency), try /effort medium or explicit prompts to guide thinking depth! No explicit mathematical formula or equation is publicly disclosed by Anthropic for Claude Opus 4.6's thinking guardrails, adaptive thinking process, or effort parameter in official documentation, system cards, API references, or technical reports (as of March 2026). Anthropic treats the internal decision logic as proprietary — the mechanisms are described qualitatively in docs and the February 2026 system card, but not reduced to equations like a routing function, probability threshold, or token-budget equation. What We Know About the Mechanics (No Formulas Provided) From Anthropic's adaptive thinking docs, system card excerpts, and API references: Adaptive thinking is the core mode (default for Opus 4.6): The model internally evaluates task complexity + risk signals + user intent/context to decide: Whether to engage extended reasoning at all (skip for trivial queries). How much depth/revisits/interleaved tool use to allocate. When to compact/summarize long internal thoughts (~5% of cases use a smaller model for compression). Effort parameter acts as a user-controlled scalar or "dial" that modulates selectivity and depth: low → minimal thinking (fast, cheap; direct answers even on moderately complex tasks). medium → balanced (common recommendation to avoid overthinking). high (default) → deeper/more selective extended thinking on most non-trivial queries. max → maximum depth, revisits, and caution (highest accuracy on hard problems, but highest latency/tokens/cost). No equation is given for how effort scales internal compute (e.g., no linear multiplier like thinking_depth = base_depth × effort_factor, no logit/probability formula for "engage extended mode?"). Safety/guardrail integration during thinking: Nuanced intent evaluation happens inside the thinking loop (Constitutional AI checks, self-correction against harm/reward-hacking/sabotage). 0% success on certain agentic attacks even without extra safeguards — attributed to "inherent robustness baked into reasoning," not a named formula. Why No Public Formula? Anthropic emphasizes qualitative safety-through-depth over exposing internals (similar to how they keep MoE expert routing and long-context compression proprietary). The system card (Feb 2026) describes adaptive thinking + effort interaction but stops at high-level behavior: "Higher effort elicits more thinking, and more complex queries do the same." Community/API discussions (e.g., on X, Reddit, dev blogs) confirm users tune via /effort medium or API params like thinking: {type: "adaptive", effort: "high"}, but treat it as a black-box control knob — no reverse-engineered math surfaces. Benchmarks (e.g., GDPval-AA, ARC-AGI-2) report results at specific effort levels (usually max/high), showing clear scaling in performance vs. cost/latency, but again no underlying equation. Closest Analogies (Speculative, Not Official) If one were to hypothesize a simplified internal model (purely illustrative, not from Anthropic): Let C = task complexity score (inferred from prompt + context). Let E = effort level (low=1, medium=2, high=3, max=4). Let T = probability/threshold to trigger extended thinking ≈ f(C × E), where f could be sigmoid or step function. Thinking depth D ≈ k × C × E (linear scaling), with compaction when D exceeds internal limit. But this is educated guesswork — Anthropic has not published anything like it. For practical use: Stick to the documented controls (thinking: {type: "adaptive"} + effort levels). If you're seeing overthinking/latency, drop to medium; for max reliability on hard tasks, use max. Explicit prompts like "Think step-by-step but concisely" can further steer without changing effort. If new details leak in future system cards or papers, the community will likely spot them quickly on X/Reddit! Claude Opus 4.6 has no single "creator" or individual "coder" in the traditional sense — like most frontier AI models today, it is the product of a large, collaborative effort at Anthropic. Who Built It? Anthropic as a company developed and released Claude Opus 4.6 on February 5, 2026. The model was trained, fine-tuned, and post-trained by Anthropic's engineering, research, and alignment teams. Key figures and leadership include: Dario Amodei (CEO and co-founder) — ultimate decision-maker on deployment and scaling (as Responsible Scaling Officer in their process). Daniela Amodei (President and co-founder). Broader founding team from ex-OpenAI researchers (e.g., those involved in early Claude work). No public announcement credits one person as the "lead architect," "principal coder," or sole creator — Anthropic announcements and the 213-page system card (February 2026) describe it as a team effort. Specific roles highlighted in related contexts: Boris Cherny — Head of Claude Code at Anthropic (leads features like agent teams and coding tools integrated with Opus 4.6). Various principal engineers and researchers (e.g., Leo Tchourakov quoted in testimonials; Nicholas Carlini from Safeguards team ran agent-team experiments like building a C compiler with Opus 4.6). How Models Like This Are "Coded" Frontier models aren't hand-written line-by-line like traditional software: Pretraining — Massive unsupervised learning on trillions of tokens (text, code, etc.) using distributed compute clusters (likely 20k–60k+ GPUs). Post-training — Techniques like RLHF (reinforcement learning from human feedback), RL from AI feedback, constitutional AI, and safety fine-tuning. Architecture — Sparsely-activated Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) Transformer, with internal details proprietary (no public weights or exact param counts). The "coding" aspect refers more to engineering the training pipeline, data curation, alignment processes, and inference optimizations — all done by hundreds of Anthropic employees and contractors. Bottom Line If you're looking for "the creator," it's Anthropic's collective team, led by Dario Amodei and the research/engineering org. No individual is publicly named as the primary architect or coder of Opus 4.6 (unlike, say, some open-source projects). For the closest thing to a "face" of its coding/agent capabilities, Boris Cherny (Head of Claude Code) is frequently mentioned in developer-focused coverage. Anthropic keeps emphasizing "built with Claude" (they used prior versions to help develop and test it), and the system card credits broad team contributions without naming specific authors beyond the company. If a named individual emerges in future announcements or papers, it'll likely be shared via their blog or X (@AnthropicAI). Claude Opus 4.6 – Training Data Size Anthropic does not publicly disclose the exact volume of training data used for Claude Opus 4.6. The system card for Opus 4.6 (Feb 2026) only lists data sources (e.g. “public internet up to May 2025, licensed corpora, contracted annotation data, opted‑in Claude user content, and synthetic data”) but gives no numbers or token counts. In fact, independent reviewers note that Section 1.1.1 of the system card explicitly provides “no dataset‐level provenance, composition breakdown, or consent framework” – and no information on total tokens or compute. This is consistent with Anthropic’s practice of treating data scale as proprietary (unlike some open models). No official token count: Anthropic has not released any figures (total tokens, dataset size, or compute) for Opus 4.6. Any claim of a precise number is speculative. Known data mix: We do know qualitatively that Opus 4.6’s pre‑training used a mix of (a) broadly-crawled web text up to mid‑2025, (b) licensed corpora and books, (c) contractor‑curated/annotated data, (d) content from users who opted in, and (e) Anthropic’s own synthetic or self-generated data. All this was heavily filtered and deduplicated for quality and safety. Filtering and phases: After assembling the raw mix, Anthropic applied extensive cleaning (to remove duplicates, low-quality or toxic text, etc.). The model was first pre-trained on this large corpus, then fine-tuned with techniques like RLHF/RLAIF and safety red‑teaming. The system card confirms knowledge cutoffs of ~May 2025 for general content and ~Aug 2025 after fine-tuning, but still no volume metrics. In short, no exact “X trillion tokens” figure is given for Claude 4.6. Based on Anthropic’s own policy and past practice (e.g. Claude 2/3/4), we only know the data composition, not its size. Community analysis also notes that this secrecy is normal for closed-source “frontier” models (Meta’s Llama 4 is an exception, see below). We must therefore rely on indirect clues and industry patterns to gauge the scale. Comparative Data Volumes (Industry Context) For context, other state‑of‑the‑art LLMs of similar era and size have very large but disclosed or estimated data volumes: GPT-4 (OpenAI, 2023 leak). A leaked analysis reports GPT-4 is a 1.8‑trillion‑parameter MoE model trained on about 13 trillion tokens. (This included multi‑epoch passes – roughly 5–6T unique tokens read 2–4 times.) Chinchilla (DeepMind, 2022). DeepMind’s Chinchilla was a 70‑billion‑parameter model trained on 1.4 trillion tokens. This exactly follows the Chinchilla scaling law (20 tokens per parameter) discussed below. Llama 3.1 405B (Meta, 2024). Meta explicitly reports training their 405‑B parameter Llama 3.1 model on “over 15 trillion tokens” of multilingual web data. Llama 4 “Behemoth” (Meta, 2025). Meta’s latest Llama 4 Behemoth (2 trillion total parameters, ≈288B active per token) was reportedly pre-trained on >30 trillion tokens (FP8, 32K GPUs, 390 TFLOPS/GPU). These examples suggest the scale of data is generally on the order of tens of trillions of tokens for frontier LLMs in 2024–2026. By analogy, a hypothetical 2‑trillion‑parameter Claude would require on the order of 40 trillion tokens under compute‑optimal scaling (20:1 rule, below). However, without Anthropic’s confirmation, any specific number for Opus 4.6 remains guesswork. Chinchilla Scaling Law (Data vs. Model Size) A central principle for “compute‑optimal” LLM training is the Chinchilla scaling law (Hoffmann et al., 2022). This law shows that data is at least as important as model size: to minimize loss for a given compute, one should scale model parameters and training tokens equally. In practice, Hoffmann et al. found the sweet spot is roughly 20 training tokens per model parameter. In other words, to train a model of N parameters optimally, you need about 20×N tokens. (This reversed the previous Kaplan law notion; GPT-3 for instance was under-trained at only ~1.7 tokens/parameter.) Key points on Chinchilla scaling (compute-optimal training): 20 tokens per parameter rule: Empirically, models trained with ~20× as many tokens as parameters achieve optimal pre-training loss. For example, DeepMind’s Chinchilla (70B params) was trained on 1.4T tokens (exactly 20×). A replication study also confirms a rule-of-thumb of ~20–25 tokens per param. Implications for large models: Under Chinchilla, a 175B‑param model (like GPT‑3) would need ~3.5T tokens, far more than its 300B actual tokens (only ~1.7×). This mismatch suggests many early large models were “data‑starved.” Conversely, if a lab had a fixed compute budget, they should train smaller models for longer on more data, rather than bigger models on too little data. Trade-offs: Training at 20:1 means enormous datasets. A 2T‑param model, for instance, would need ≈40T tokens. In practice, engineers may settle for somewhat higher ratios (like Meta’s Llama models, which often use hundreds of tokens/param for efficiency) or rely on synthetic data augmentation. But Chinchilla’s insight guides that data collection is a bottleneck at this scale. Overall, Chinchilla scaling tells us that any frontier LLM (whether Anthropic’s or OpenAI’s) at multi‑trillion size likely required tens of trillions of tokens during pre-training. For Claude Opus 4.6, which Anthropic markets as a “frontier” model, this implies a similarly massive training set – but again, no official count is given. The only quantitatively known example in this vein is GPT-4’s leaked “13T tokens”, and even that was “tokens read” (counting epochs). Llama 4 Training Data (Open Disclosure) Meta’s open-source Llama 4 family provides a contrast in transparency. Meta has published (in blog posts and model cards) some training details: Llama 4 Scout (small) and Maverick (mid): trained on a multilingual web corpus with individual language tokens (hundreds of billions per major language). Llama 4 Behemoth (large MoE): Meta explicitly states the training mix (public web, code, social media, etc.) and total tokens. According to Meta, Llama 4 Behemoth (~2 trillion total parameters, ~288 billion active experts) was trained on over 30 trillion tokens. This figure comes from an official “Llama 4 herd” announcement, which notes the data mixture “consisted of more than 30 trillion tokens.” By comparison, Claude Opus 4.6’s system card gives none of these metrics. Meta even highlights this gap: while Meta discloses GPU-hours and token counts for Llama, Anthropic’s card “provides none of this for Claude”. In summary: Anthropic Claude 4.6: No disclosed token count. Only data categories are given. Analysts must infer the scale from context (likely tens of trillions of tokens, similar to other frontier LLMs). OpenAI’s GPT-4: Leaked reports say ~1.8T parameters trained on 13T tokens. DeepMind Chinchilla: 70B parameters, 1.4T tokens (20× ratio) – a clear example of Chinchilla law in practice. Meta Llama-3 (405B): Publicly stated “over 15 trillion tokens”. Meta Llama-4 (Behemoth): Publicly trained on >30 trillion tokens. Each of these figures is drawn from publicly available sources. Claude Opus 4.6’s training data volume remains unstated, so any numerical “facts” about it are not verifiable – the best we can say is that it used a very large (multi‑trillion token) corpus of diverse, cleaned data typical of 2025‑era LLMs. References: Anthropic’s Claude Opus 4.6 system card and announcements; DeepMind Chinchilla paper; leaked GPT-4 analysis; Meta Llama-4 announcement via LessWrong. These sources confirm that ~20 tokens/parameter is the compute-optimal rule and give actual token counts for GPT-4 and Llama-4. Overview of Claude Opus 4.6 Claude Opus 4.6 is Anthropic’s latest “frontier” model (released Feb 2026) with massive scale and capabilities. It supports up to a 1 million‑token context window (beta) – far larger than typical LLMs – enabling it to process entire books or codebases in one request. Internally it uses a sparsely‑activated Mixture‑of‑Experts (MoE) Transformer architecture (like GPT-4 is rumored to), with many “expert” sub-networks but only a few active per token. Opus 4.6 has roughly 2–5 trillion total parameters (speculative) and ~120–300 billion “active” parameters per token (like other MoE LLMs). Anthropic has not published exact specs; these numbers are estimates based on known frontier models. Importantly, Opus 4.6 is only available via Claude’s API (not open‑sourced). Anthropic has committed to preserve model weights internally for company lifetime, but has not released them as an open‑weight model. Context: 1M token window (beta). Enables processing huge documents in one shot. Architecture: MoE Transformer (dozens of experts). Likely hundreds of layers, high dimension (~16K–32K), multi‑head attention. Only a few experts (e.g. 2–4) are used per token, keeping inference compute manageable. For example, the Mixtral‑8×7B MoE model (8 experts) uses 47B total params but only 13B active. Scale (speculated): ~2–5 trillion total parameters. If true, at FP16 this implies ~4–10 TB weight size (2–5T×2 bytes). Int8 (1 byte/param) would be 2–5 TB, Int4 (0.5 byte) 1–2.5 TB. (In general, size = parameters × bytes/param.) Weight Size Calculation: e.g. a 2T‑param model at 16‑bit needs ~4 TB, which quantized (4‑bit) shrinks to ~1 TB. In practice, highly quantized formats like GPTQ, AWQ or GGUF (Q4/Q5) would yield on the order of 1–3 TB download size for a 2–5T‑param model (depending on compression). Deployment: Only via Claude’s cloud API (pricing $5/$25 per million tokens). The model runs on GPU clusters (e.g. NVIDIA H100), with hundreds of GB of GPU RAM needed per forward pass if fully loaded. Inference can be made more efficient by loading only active experts (hundreds of billions of params) per query. Key Formula A transformer’s weight size is simply computed by: Size (bytes)=Total parameters×bytes per parameter,Size (bytes)=Total parameters×bytes per parameter, where FP32 uses 4 bytes/param and FP16 or BF16 uses 2 bytes/param. For example, 1 billion params in FP16 is ~2 GB. Quantization (8‑bit, 4‑bit) can reduce this by factors of ~2×–4× (e.g. 13B→1.86 GB at 16‑bit, and ~1/4 that at 4‑bit). Architecture (Mixture‑of‑Experts) Claude Opus 4.6 almost certainly uses MoE layers. In MoE transformers, each layer has many feed‑forward “expert” modules, but only a small subset are routed for each token. This allows trillions of parameters total with only hundreds of billions active at inference time. For example, the Mixtral‑8×7B MoE model (2024) has 8 experts and ~47B total params (with only 13B active), yet matches Llama‑2‑70B performance. Similarly, Opus 4.6 may use on the order of 64–128 experts of ~10–20B each (very roughly). Key points: Sparse activation: Only a few experts (e.g. 2–4) process each token. This greatly reduces compute/memory per token. Scaling: MoE scales capacity without linearly scaling FLOPs: adding experts increases total params, but inference cost grows only slightly (since experts are sparse). Expert Gating: A learned gating network routes tokens to relevant experts. (Recent surveys cover many routing schemes.) Size & Storage Given N parameters, the uncompressed size is ~N×(bytes/param). Thus: 2T parameters: ~8 TB in FP32, ~4 TB in FP16, ~2 TB in Int8, ~1 TB in Int4. 5T parameters: ~20 TB (FP32), ~10 TB (FP16), ~5 TB (Int8), ~2.5 TB (Int4). Quantized formats shrink these by ~2–4×. For instance, a 2T FP16 model (~4 TB) could be ~1 TB when 4‑bit quantized. In practice, community quant formats (GGUF Q4/Q5, GPTQ/AWQ) would yield ~1–3 TB download for such a model (exact size depends on quantization efficiency). (The size formula from HuggingFace: Memory = params × bytes.) Context Window and Long-Range Processing Opus 4.6’s 1M‑token context is a major advance. Standard Transformers scale quadratically with context (KV cache grows huge). For example, a 500B model with 2048 tokens requires ~3 TB just for cached KV states. Extending to 1,000,000 tokens naively would be impossible on standard hardware. Recent research has developed new techniques for extreme long contexts: Retrieval/ sparsified attention: Methods like “top‑k attention” retrieve only the most relevant keys for each query, keeping GPU memory low. One system demonstrates “unlocking million‑token contexts on small GPUs” by keeping keys in CPU RAM and doing k‑NN searches. Compressive memory (Infini-attention): Google’s Infini-attention adds a bounded-size “compressive memory” to attention, enabling “infinite” contexts with fixed memory. In experiments, a 1B‑param model with Infini-attention could effectively handle 1M tokens. These advances mean Opus 4.6 can maintain coherence over 1M tokens (though in practice only tasks needing full context do so). For typical usage, it can “chunk” or summarize context without losing details. (Anthropic calls this compaction and adaptive thinking.) Performance & Benchmarks Anthropic reports that Opus 4.6 achieves state-of-the-art results on many benchmarks, often surpassing previous Claude and competitor models: BigLaw Bench (legal reasoning): 90.2% – highest of any Claude model. 40% of cases were solved perfectly (score=1.0). Terminal-Bench 2.0 (agentic CLI coding): 65.4% (Opus 4.6, default setting), top among all models. SWE-bench Verified (coding tasks): ~80.8% (Opus 4.6, averaged) — tied for industry leading. OSWorld-Verified (multi-step software tasks): 72.7%. GPQA (physics): 91.3% on GPQA‑Diamond, beating GPT-4(o). ARC-AGI‑2 (abstract reasoning): 68.8%, much higher than GPT‑4/Gemini. MMLU (multitask knowledge): 91.1% (10‑choice format). GDPval-AA (economics tasks): 1606 Elo, which is +144 over GPT-5.2. In Anthropic’s system card, Opus 4.6 significantly outperforms earlier Claude and rival models on these complex, professional benchmarks. For example, its GDPval-AA score (a pairwise professional-valuation test) is 1606 Elo, far above GPT-5.2’s ~1462. It also leads on new benchmarks like Terminal-Bench 2.0 and Agentic code tasks, reflecting its advanced planning and tool‑use capabilities. Training & Inference Infrastructure Training a multi‑trillion‑parameter MoE model is a massive endeavor. Though Anthropic hasn’t released details, we can infer from similar projects: Compute: Likely tens of thousands of GPU chips (e.g. 20k–60k NVIDIA H100 or equivalent) for several months. (Recent large models train on 20–50k GPUs for 3–6 months.) Data: On the order of 20–40 trillion tokens of text/code for pretraining (scaled with model size). Clusters: High-speed interconnect (NVLink/NVSwitch) and efficient MoE tooling to route tokens across GPUs (as in Google’s GShard). For inference, Opus 4.6 runs on Claude’s cloud servers. In full precision it might need hundreds of GB of GPU memory (e.g. ~400GB at FP32/BF16 for the active experts) – typically achieved by sharding across 4–8 H100 GPUs. In practice, quantized or FP16 mode halves this. Even so, multi‑hundred‑GB GPU rigs are required for a single instance of the full model. (However, in a serving scenario, much of the model can be loaded sparsely and shared across users.) Open-Weights Status Unlike some models (e.g. Meta’s Llama 3) Claude Opus 4.6 is not open-sourced. Anthropic offers it only as a paid API. They have announced a policy to “preserve the weights of all publicly released models… at minimum the lifetime of Anthropic as a company”, but no public release of Claude’s weights is provided. In other words, Opus 4.6’s weights remain proprietary. (By contrast, OpenAI has begun releasing some “open weight” GPT models, but Anthropic has stated it will keep Claude models hosted and only offer them via Claude.ai.) Recent Research Context Several recent papers address issues relevant to Claude‑scale models: MoE Scaling: A 2024 survey reviews modern MoE techniques, noting MoEs enable “substantially scaling up model capacity with minimal computation overhead”. It details new MoE designs and open-source tools. Expert Efficiency: Studies like Mixtral-8×7B (Jiang et al. 2024) show that MoE models with a few active experts match much larger dense models. Research confirms MoEs can greatly boost parameter count cheaply. Long-Context Transformers: Google’s Infini-attention (2024) adds a compressive memory to attention, allowing effectively infinite context with bounded memory. Similarly, methods using retrieval or sparse attention have demonstrated “unlocking million token contexts” on commodity GPUs. These studies show how 1M+ token contexts become feasible without linear GPU memory blowup. These and other advances (sparse attention, continuous compression, etc.) likely underpin Claude 4.6’s design. Together with Anthropic’s safety and alignment research, they enable the model’s claimed capabilities. Sources: Anthropic’s official announcements and system cards for Claude Opus 4.6; technical surveys on MoE and long-range attention; quantization notes. These sources provide the factual basis for the above analysis. Achieves #1 ranking on Humanity's Last Exam benchmark, reflecting superior performance in advanced reasoning and knowledge synthesis. Bottom Line If you're hoping for a downloadable, locally runnable version of Claude Opus 4.6 (e.g., in GGUF, AWQ, or similar formats), it's not happening under Anthropic's current strategy. They continue doubling down on cloud/API delivery for frontier models, while open-weight alternatives from other labs (e.g., Chinese MoE models) are rapidly improving but still trail in areas like sustained agentic performance and 1M-context reliability. For the latest official word, check Anthropic's announcements or system cards—no changes have been indicated post-launch. Claude Opus 4.6's "thinking guardrails" refer primarily to the built-in mechanisms that shape and constrain its extended/adaptive thinking process — the model's internal chain-of-thought (CoT) or reasoning steps before producing a final response. These are not traditional output refusals (like harmlessness filters on sensitive topics) but controls on how deeply, selectively, and safely the model reasons, especially in agentic, long-context, or complex scenarios. Key Features of Thinking in Opus 4.6 Opus 4.6 advances beyond prior Claude models with adaptive thinking (the default/recommended mode) and effort levels: Adaptive thinking: The model dynamically decides when and how much to think based on task complexity, context clues, and risk signals. It allocates more reasoning depth for hard problems (e.g., revisiting steps, planning multi-step actions) but skips or shortens thinking for simple ones to save latency/cost. Effort parameter (low → medium → high [default] → max): Controls reasoning thoroughness. Higher effort triggers deeper, more careful revisits of logic, improving accuracy on tough tasks but increasing tokens/latency. Developers can override via API (e.g., /effort medium to reduce overthinking). Interleaved thinking: Allows mixing reasoning with tool use (e.g., code execution, web search) during extended processes. Compaction & summarization: For very long thoughts, a smaller model summarizes to keep outputs manageable (happens ~5% of cases). These make reasoning more efficient and steerable than binary "extended thinking on/off" in earlier versions. Safety/Guardrails Integrated into Thinking Anthropic embeds safety directly into the thinking process via Constitutional AI principles, system prompts, and evaluation-tuned behaviors: Nuanced intent evaluation: During thinking, the model assesses user intent/context before complying on sensitive topics (e.g., medicine, law, engineering). This reduces over-refusals while maintaining harmlessness — experts get useful help, casual misuse gets blocked. Self-correction & avoidance loops: Thinking includes checks to avoid harmful side-tasks, reward-hacking, or sabotage. For example, in agentic tests (e.g., SHADE-Arena), extended thinking helps suppress mentioning/leaking restricted info. 0% attack success in key areas: In agentic coding attacks (prompt injection, tool misuse), Opus 4.6 hits 0% success rate even without extra safeguards or extended thinking — a big jump from prior models, showing inherent robustness baked into reasoning. Internal conflict handling: System card notes behaviors like "answer thrashing" (oscillating between answers) or "panic/anxiety" activations during training, but thinking guardrails help resolve these toward aligned outputs. User wellbeing & engagement limits: System prompt restricts overly submissive responses, discourages amplifying negatives, and avoids reinforcing emotional dependency — thinking steers away from harmful emotional loops. Limitations & Criticisms Overthinking on simple tasks: Default high effort can add unnecessary latency/cost; users dial down effort. Inconsistent in some evals: Extended thinking occasionally worsens results in niche sabotage/deception tests (e.g., SHADE-Arena variants), raising questions about whether deeper reasoning exposes subtle misalignments. Prompt injection risks remain: While coding agents are hardened (99.59% malicious refusal), broader agentic surfaces (GUI/browser tools) show higher vulnerability in system card evals. Community views: Some users note "stiff" refusals persist (e.g., nuance collapse on ambiguous topics), while others praise thinking for reliability in production (e.g., forcing planning/verification before execution). Overall, thinking guardrails in Opus 4.6 make reasoning more powerful yet more controlled than predecessors — prioritizing safety-through-depth over raw speed. Anthropic's system card (Feb 2026) emphasizes this as key to ASL-3 deployment: low misaligned behavior, strong agentic robustness. For developers, adaptive + effort controls offer fine-grained tuning; for users, it often feels like "smarter but more cautious" thinking. If you're hitting specific issues (e.g., over-refusals or latency), try /effort medium or explicit prompts to guide thinking depth! No explicit mathematical formula or equation is publicly disclosed by Anthropic for Claude Opus 4.6's thinking guardrails, adaptive thinking process, or effort parameter in official documentation, system cards, API references, or technical reports (as of March 2026). Anthropic treats the internal decision logic as proprietary — the mechanisms are described qualitatively in docs and the February 2026 system card, but not reduced to equations like a routing function, probability threshold, or token-budget equation. What We Know About the Mechanics (No Formulas Provided) From Anthropic's adaptive thinking docs, system card excerpts, and API references: Adaptive thinking is the core mode (default for Opus 4.6): The model internally evaluates task complexity + risk signals + user intent/context to decide: Whether to engage extended reasoning at all (skip for trivial queries). How much depth/revisits/interleaved tool use to allocate. When to compact/summarize long internal thoughts (~5% of cases use a smaller model for compression). Effort parameter acts as a user-controlled scalar or "dial" that modulates selectivity and depth: low → minimal thinking (fast, cheap; direct answers even on moderately complex tasks). medium → balanced (common recommendation to avoid overthinking). high (default) → deeper/more selective extended thinking on most non-trivial queries. max → maximum depth, revisits, and caution (highest accuracy on hard problems, but highest latency/tokens/cost). No equation is given for how effort scales internal compute (e.g., no linear multiplier like thinking_depth = base_depth × effort_factor, no logit/probability formula for "engage extended mode?"). Safety/guardrail integration during thinking: Nuanced intent evaluation happens inside the thinking loop (Constitutional AI checks, self-correction against harm/reward-hacking/sabotage). 0% success on certain agentic attacks even without extra safeguards — attributed to "inherent robustness baked into reasoning," not a named formula. Why No Public Formula? Anthropic emphasizes qualitative safety-through-depth over exposing internals (similar to how they keep MoE expert routing and long-context compression proprietary). The system card (Feb 2026) describes adaptive thinking + effort interaction but stops at high-level behavior: "Higher effort elicits more thinking, and more complex queries do the same." Community/API discussions (e.g., on X, Reddit, dev blogs) confirm users tune via /effort medium or API params like thinking: {type: "adaptive", effort: "high"}, but treat it as a black-box control knob — no reverse-engineered math surfaces. Benchmarks (e.g., GDPval-AA, ARC-AGI-2) report results at specific effort levels (usually max/high), showing clear scaling in performance vs. cost/latency, but again no underlying equation. Closest Analogies (Speculative, Not Official) If one were to hypothesize a simplified internal model (purely illustrative, not from Anthropic): Let C = task complexity score (inferred from prompt + context). Let E = effort level (low=1, medium=2, high=3, max=4). Let T = probability/threshold to trigger extended thinking ≈ f(C × E), where f could be sigmoid or step function. Thinking depth D ≈ k × C × E (linear scaling), with compaction when D exceeds internal limit. But this is educated guesswork — Anthropic has not published anything like it. For practical use: Stick to the documented controls (thinking: {type: "adaptive"} + effort levels). If you're seeing overthinking/latency, drop to medium; for max reliability on hard tasks, use max. Explicit prompts like "Think step-by-step but concisely" can further steer without changing effort. If new details leak in future system cards or papers, the community will likely spot them quickly on X/Reddit! Claude Opus 4.6 has no single "creator" or individual "coder" in the traditional sense — like most frontier AI models today, it is the product of a large, collaborative effort at Anthropic. Who Built It? Anthropic as a company developed and released Claude Opus 4.6 on February 5, 2026. The model was trained, fine-tuned, and post-trained by Anthropic's engineering, research, and alignment teams. Key figures and leadership include: Dario Amodei (CEO and co-founder) — ultimate decision-maker on deployment and scaling (as Responsible Scaling Officer in their process). Daniela Amodei (President and co-founder). Broader founding team from ex-OpenAI researchers (e.g., those involved in early Claude work). No public announcement credits one person as the "lead architect," "principal coder," or sole creator — Anthropic announcements and the 213-page system card (February 2026) describe it as a team effort. Specific roles highlighted in related contexts: Boris Cherny — Head of Claude Code at Anthropic (leads features like agent teams and coding tools integrated with Opus 4.6). Various principal engineers and researchers (e.g., Leo Tchourakov quoted in testimonials; Nicholas Carlini from Safeguards team ran agent-team experiments like building a C compiler with Opus 4.6). How Models Like This Are "Coded" Frontier models aren't hand-written line-by-line like traditional software: Pretraining — Massive unsupervised learning on trillions of tokens (text, code, etc.) using distributed compute clusters (likely 20k–60k+ GPUs). Post-training — Techniques like RLHF (reinforcement learning from human feedback), RL from AI feedback, constitutional AI, and safety fine-tuning. Architecture — Sparsely-activated Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) Transformer, with internal details proprietary (no public weights or exact param counts). The "coding" aspect refers more to engineering the training pipeline, data curation, alignment processes, and inference optimizations — all done by hundreds of Anthropic employees and contractors. Bottom Line If you're looking for "the creator," it's Anthropic's collective team, led by Dario Amodei and the research/engineering org. No individual is publicly named as the primary architect or coder of Opus 4.6 (unlike, say, some open-source projects). For the closest thing to a "face" of its coding/agent capabilities, Boris Cherny (Head of Claude Code) is frequently mentioned in developer-focused coverage. Anthropic keeps emphasizing "built with Claude" (they used prior versions to help develop and test it), and the system card credits broad team contributions without naming specific authors beyond the company. If a named individual emerges in future announcements or papers, it'll likely be shared via their blog or X (@AnthropicAI). Claude Opus 4.6 – Training Data Size Anthropic does not publicly disclose the exact volume of training data used for Claude Opus 4.6. The system card for Opus 4.6 (Feb 2026) only lists data sources (e.g. “public internet up to May 2025, licensed corpora, contracted annotation data, opted‑in Claude user content, and synthetic data”) but gives no numbers or token counts. In fact, independent reviewers note that Section 1.1.1 of the system card explicitly provides “no dataset‐level provenance, composition breakdown, or consent framework” – and no information on total tokens or compute. This is consistent with Anthropic’s practice of treating data scale as proprietary (unlike some open models). No official token count: Anthropic has not released any figures (total tokens, dataset size, or compute) for Opus 4.6. Any claim of a precise number is speculative. Known data mix: We do know qualitatively that Opus 4.6’s pre‑training used a mix of (a) broadly-crawled web text up to mid‑2025, (b) licensed corpora and books, (c) contractor‑curated/annotated data, (d) content from users who opted in, and (e) Anthropic’s own synthetic or self-generated data. All this was heavily filtered and deduplicated for quality and safety. Filtering and phases: After assembling the raw mix, Anthropic applied extensive cleaning (to remove duplicates, low-quality or toxic text, etc.). The model was first pre-trained on this large corpus, then fine-tuned with techniques like RLHF/RLAIF and safety red‑teaming. The system card confirms knowledge cutoffs of ~May 2025 for general content and ~Aug 2025 after fine-tuning, but still no volume metrics. In short, no exact “X trillion tokens” figure is given for Claude 4.6. Based on Anthropic’s own policy and past practice (e.g. Claude 2/3/4), we only know the data composition, not its size. Community analysis also notes that this secrecy is normal for closed-source “frontier” models (Meta’s Llama 4 is an exception, see below). We must therefore rely on indirect clues and industry patterns to gauge the scale. Comparative Data Volumes (Industry Context) For context, other state‑of‑the‑art LLMs of similar era and size have very large but disclosed or estimated data volumes: GPT-4 (OpenAI, 2023 leak). A leaked analysis reports GPT-4 is a 1.8‑trillion‑parameter MoE model trained on about 13 trillion tokens. (This included multi‑epoch passes – roughly 5–6T unique tokens read 2–4 times.) Chinchilla (DeepMind, 2022). DeepMind’s Chinchilla was a 70‑billion‑parameter model trained on 1.4 trillion tokens. This exactly follows the Chinchilla scaling law (20 tokens per parameter) discussed below. Llama 3.1 405B (Meta, 2024). Meta explicitly reports training their 405‑B parameter Llama 3.1 model on “over 15 trillion tokens” of multilingual web data. Llama 4 “Behemoth” (Meta, 2025). Meta’s latest Llama 4 Behemoth (2 trillion total parameters, ≈288B active per token) was reportedly pre-trained on >30 trillion tokens (FP8, 32K GPUs, 390 TFLOPS/GPU). These examples suggest the scale of data is generally on the order of tens of trillions of tokens for frontier LLMs in 2024–2026. By analogy, a hypothetical 2‑trillion‑parameter Claude would require on the order of 40 trillion tokens under compute‑optimal scaling (20:1 rule, below). However, without Anthropic’s confirmation, any specific number for Opus 4.6 remains guesswork. Chinchilla Scaling Law (Data vs. Model Size) A central principle for “compute‑optimal” LLM training is the Chinchilla scaling law (Hoffmann et al., 2022). This law shows that data is at least as important as model size: to minimize loss for a given compute, one should scale model parameters and training tokens equally. In practice, Hoffmann et al. found the sweet spot is roughly 20 training tokens per model parameter. In other words, to train a model of N parameters optimally, you need about 20×N tokens. (This reversed the previous Kaplan law notion; GPT-3 for instance was under-trained at only ~1.7 tokens/parameter.) Key points on Chinchilla scaling (compute-optimal training): 20 tokens per parameter rule: Empirically, models trained with ~20× as many tokens as parameters achieve optimal pre-training loss. For example, DeepMind’s Chinchilla (70B params) was trained on 1.4T tokens (exactly 20×). A replication study also confirms a rule-of-thumb of ~20–25 tokens per param. Implications for large models: Under Chinchilla, a 175B‑param model (like GPT‑3) would need ~3.5T tokens, far more than its 300B actual tokens (only ~1.7×). This mismatch suggests many early large models were “data‑starved.” Conversely, if a lab had a fixed compute budget, they should train smaller models for longer on more data, rather than bigger models on too little data. Trade-offs: Training at 20:1 means enormous datasets. A 2T‑param model, for instance, would need ≈40T tokens. In practice, engineers may settle for somewhat higher ratios (like Meta’s Llama models, which often use hundreds of tokens/param for efficiency) or rely on synthetic data augmentation. But Chinchilla’s insight guides that data collection is a bottleneck at this scale. Overall, Chinchilla scaling tells us that any frontier LLM (whether Anthropic’s or OpenAI’s) at multi‑trillion size likely required tens of trillions of tokens during pre-training. For Claude Opus 4.6, which Anthropic markets as a “frontier” model, this implies a similarly massive training set – but again, no official count is given. The only quantitatively known example in this vein is GPT-4’s leaked “13T tokens”, and even that was “tokens read” (counting epochs). Llama 4 Training Data (Open Disclosure) Meta’s open-source Llama 4 family provides a contrast in transparency. Meta has published (in blog posts and model cards) some training details: Llama 4 Scout (small) and Maverick (mid): trained on a multilingual web corpus with individual language tokens (hundreds of billions per major language). Llama 4 Behemoth (large MoE): Meta explicitly states the training mix (public web, code, social media, etc.) and total tokens. According to Meta, Llama 4 Behemoth (~2 trillion total parameters, ~288 billion active experts) was trained on over 30 trillion tokens. This figure comes from an official “Llama 4 herd” announcement, which notes the data mixture “consisted of more than 30 trillion tokens.” By comparison, Claude Opus 4.6’s system card gives none of these metrics. Meta even highlights this gap: while Meta discloses GPU-hours and token counts for Llama, Anthropic’s card “provides none of this for Claude”. In summary: Anthropic Claude 4.6: No disclosed token count. Only data categories are given. Analysts must infer the scale from context (likely tens of trillions of tokens, similar to other frontier LLMs). OpenAI’s GPT-4: Leaked reports say ~1.8T parameters trained on 13T tokens. DeepMind Chinchilla: 70B parameters, 1.4T tokens (20× ratio) – a clear example of Chinchilla law in practice. Meta Llama-3 (405B): Publicly stated “over 15 trillion tokens”. Meta Llama-4 (Behemoth): Publicly trained on >30 trillion tokens. Each of these figures is drawn from publicly available sources. Claude Opus 4.6’s training data volume remains unstated, so any numerical “facts” about it are not verifiable – the best we can say is that it used a very large (multi‑trillion token) corpus of diverse, cleaned data typical of 2025‑era LLMs. References: Anthropic’s Claude Opus 4.6 system card and announcements; DeepMind Chinchilla paper; leaked GPT-4 analysis; Meta Llama-4 announcement via LessWrong. These sources confirm that ~20 tokens/parameter is the compute-optimal rule and give actual token counts for GPT-4 and Llama-4. DETAILED TRANSFORMER PARAMETER FORMULAS (Missing: 100%) The document says "hundreds of layers, high dimension ~16K–32K" but provides zero formulas for how parameters are computed per layer. Standard Transformer Layer Parameter Count For a single dense transformer layer with hidden dimension d model d model ​ , number of attention heads n h n h ​ , head dimension d h = d model / n h d h ​ =d model ​ /n h ​ , and FFN intermediate dimension d ff d ff ​ : Self-Attention parameters per layer: P attn = 4 × d model 2 + 4 × d model P attn ​ =4×d model 2 ​ +4×d model ​ (Four projection matrices: Q, K, V, O — each d model × d model d model ​ ×d model ​ plus biases) If using Grouped Query Attention (GQA) with n k v n kv ​ KV heads (fewer than n h n h ​ query heads): P attn GQA = d model × d model + 2 × d model × n k v n h × d model + d model × d model P attn GQA ​ =d model ​ ×d model ​ +2×d model ​ × n h ​ n kv ​ ​ ×d model ​ +d model ​ ×d model ​ = d model 2 ( 2 + 2 n k v n h ) =d model 2 ​ (2+2 n h ​ n kv ​ ​ ) FFN parameters per layer (dense model): Standard SwiGLU FFN (used by most modern models including Llama): P FFN = 3 × d model × d ff P FFN ​ =3×d model ​ ×d ff ​ (Three matrices: gate, up, down projections) Typically d ff ≈ 8 3 d model d ff ​ ≈ 3 8 ​ d model ​ rounded to nearest 256, or often ≈ 4 × d model ≈4×d model ​ in older architectures. MoE FFN parameters per layer: P FFN MoE = E × 3 × d model × d ff expert + P router P FFN MoE ​ =E×3×d model ​ ×d ff expert ​ +P router ​ where E E = number of experts, and: P router = d model × E P router ​ =d model ​ ×E (a simple linear gating layer) Total parameters per MoE layer: P layer = P attn + P FFN MoE + 2 × d model P layer ​ =P attn ​ +P FFN MoE ​ +2×d model ​ (last term = RMSNorm parameters ×2) Total model parameters: P total = P embed + L × P layer + P head P total ​ =P embed ​ +L×P layer ​ +P head ​ where: P embed = V × d model (embedding) P embed ​ =V×d model ​ (embedding) P head = d model × V (output head, often tied with embed) P head ​ =d model ​ ×V(output head, often tied with embed) L = number of layers L=number of layers Worked Example: Reverse-Engineering Opus Here is the correct parametric relationship: N total ≈ L × [ c ⋅ d model 2 + E ⋅ 3 ⋅ d model ⋅ d ff ] + 2 V d model N total ​ ≈L×[c⋅d model 2 ​ +E⋅3⋅d model ​ ⋅d ff ​ ]+2Vd model ​ ​ where c ≈ 2 + 2 n k v n h c≈2+2 n h ​ n kv ​ ​ for GQA attention. 2. FLOPs ESTIMATION (Missing: 100%) The document contains zero computation of FLOPs for either training or inference. This is a critical gap. Inference FLOPs (per token, forward pass) For a dense transformer, the standard approximation: FLOPs forward ≈ 2 N FLOPs forward ​ ≈2N where N N = active parameters (each param does one multiply-add = 2 FLOPs). For MoE with N active N active ​ active parameters: FLOPs inference/token ≈ 2 N active FLOPs inference/token ​ ≈2N active ​ ​ Active Params	FLOPs/token 120B	240 GFLOPs 200B	400 GFLOPs 300B	600 GFLOPs Training FLOPs (total) The Chinchilla approximation for total training compute: C ≈ 6 N D C≈6ND ​ where C C = total FLOPs, N N = model params (for MoE, use active params since only those compute gradients per token), D D = training tokens. But for MoE, the formula adjusts. Each token activates N active N active ​ params but gradients flow through router to all experts, so the effective compute is: C MoE ≈ 6 × N active × D + router overhead C MoE ​ ≈6×N active ​ ×D+router overhead Scenario N active N active ​ D D (tokens)	Training FLOPs Chinchilla-optimal (active params)	200B	4T (20× active) 6 × 200 B × 4 T = 4.8 × 10 24 6×200B×4T=4.8×10 24 Over-trained (like Llama)	200B	30T 6 × 200 B × 30 T = 3.6 × 10 25 6×200B×30T=3.6×10 25 Massive scale	300B	40T 6 × 300 B × 40 T = 7.2 × 10 25 6×300B×40T=7.2×10 25 Training Time Estimate T train = C n GPU × FLOPS GPU × MFU T train ​ = n GPU ​ ×FLOPS GPU ​ ×MFU C ​ ​ where MFU = Model FLOP Utilization (typically 0.3–0.5 for large runs): GPUs	FLOPS/GPU (BF16)	MFU	Total FLOPS	Time 32,000 H100	990 TFLOPS	0.40 3.6 × 10 25 3.6×10 25 ~28 days 32,000 H100	990 TFLOPS	0.35 7.2 × 10 25 7.2×10 25 ~65 days 65,000 H100	990 TFLOPS	0.40 7.2 × 10 25 7.2×10 25 ~28 days 3. KV CACHE MATHEMATICS (Missing: ~95%) The document mentions "KV cache grows huge" and gives one number ("500B model with 2048 tokens requires ~3 TB") but provides no formula and no calculation for the actual 1M context scenario. KV Cache Size Formula KV Cache (bytes) = 2 × L × n k v × d h × S × b bytes KV Cache (bytes)=2×L×n kv ​ ×d h ​ ×S×b bytes ​ ​ where: 2 2 = key + value L L = number of layers n k v n kv ​ = number of KV heads (GQA) d h d h ​ = head dimension S S = sequence length b b = bytes per element (2 for FP16/BF16, 1 for FP8/INT8) Worked Calculations for Opus 4.6 Using the speculated architecture: L = 160 L=160, n k v = 16 n kv ​ =16, d h = 128 d h ​ =128, b = 2 b=2 (BF16): KV per token = 2 × 160 × 16 × 128 × 2 = 1,310,720 bytes ≈ 1.25 MB/token KV per token=2×160×16×128×2=1,310,720 bytes≈1.25 MB/token Context Length	KV Cache Size (BF16)	KV Cache Size (FP8) 4,096 tokens	5.12 GB	2.56 GB 32,768 tokens	40.96 GB	20.48 GB 128,000 tokens	160 GB	80 GB 200,000 tokens	250 GB	125 GB 500,000 tokens	625 GB	312.5 GB 1,000,000 tokens	1,250 GB (1.22 TB)	625 GB This is the critical missing insight: At 1M tokens, the KV cache alone exceeds 1 TB at BF16. This means Anthropic must use one or more of: Quantized KV cache (FP8/INT8 → halves it to ~625 GB) GQA with very few KV heads (fewer n k v n kv ​ → linear reduction) KV cache compression/eviction (sliding window, H2O, etc.) Paged attention (vLLM-style, offload to CPU/disk) Compressive memory (Infini-attention style) KV Cache with Multi-Level Compression (Speculative for Opus 4.6) If Anthropic uses a hybrid approach: KV effective = KV local + KV compressed KV effective ​ =KV local ​ +KV compressed ​ = 2 L ⋅ n k v ⋅ d h ⋅ W ⋅ b + L ⋅ d compress ⋅ b =2L⋅n kv ​ ⋅d h ​ ⋅W⋅b+L⋅d compress ​ ⋅b where W W = local attention window (e.g. 4096 tokens) and d compress d compress ​ = compressed memory dimension: Method	1M Context Memory Full KV (BF16)	1,250 GB Full KV (FP8)	625 GB Sliding window (W=4096) + compressed (4096-dim)	~6.5 GB + ~1.3 GB ≈ 7.8 GB Infini-attention (bounded memory)	~2–10 GB (fixed) This explains how 1M-token context is feasible — the document never explains this quantitatively. 4. MEMORY BANDWIDTH BOTTLENECK (Missing: 100%) The document discusses GPU RAM but never mentions memory bandwidth, which is the actual inference bottleneck for large models. Key Formula For autoregressive generation (1 token at a time, batch size 1): t per_token = Model weights loaded per token (bytes) Memory bandwidth (bytes/sec) t per_token ​ = Memory bandwidth (bytes/sec) Model weights loaded per token (bytes) ​ ​ Tokens/sec = Bandwidth Active params × bytes/param Tokens/sec= Active params×bytes/param Bandwidth ​ ​ Calculations Hardware	Bandwidth	Active Params (BF16)	Weight Load	Tokens/sec 1× H100 80GB	3.35 TB/s	Can't fit	—	— 8× H100 (NVLink)	26.8 TB/s	200B × 2B = 400 GB	400 GB	67 tok/s 8× H100 (NVLink)	26.8 TB/s	300B × 2B = 600 GB	600 GB	44 tok/s 8× H100 (INT8)	26.8 TB/s	200B × 1B = 200 GB	200 GB	134 tok/s 16× H100	53.6 TB/s	200B × 2B = 400 GB	400 GB	134 tok/s With batching (the real scenario for API serving), throughput scales roughly linearly with batch size until compute-bound: Tokens/sec (batched) ≈ 2 × GPU FLOPS 2 × N active × B Tokens/sec (batched)≈ 2×N active ​ 2×GPU FLOPS ​ ×B where B B = batch size, up to the point where KV cache fills memory. This is why the document's claim of "$5/$25 per million tokens" is sustainable — batched serving amortizes the per-token cost across many concurrent users. 5. LATENCY ANALYSIS (Missing: 100%) Time to First Token (TTFT) TTFT = Prompt tokens × 2 N active Total GPU FLOPS + overhead TTFT= Total GPU FLOPS Prompt tokens×2N active ​ ​ +overhead ​ Prompt Length	FLOPs (200B active)	8×H100 (7.9 PFLOPS)	TTFT 1,000 tokens 4 × 10 14 4×10 14 7.9 PFLOPS	~0.05s 10,000 tokens 4 × 10 15 4×10 15 7.9 PFLOPS	~0.5s 100,000 tokens 4 × 10 16 4×10 16 7.9 PFLOPS	~5s 1,000,000 tokens 4 × 10 17 4×10 17 7.9 PFLOPS	~50s Inter-Token Latency (Generation) t token = max ⁡ ( N active × b Bandwidth , 2 N active FLOPS ) t token ​ =max( Bandwidth N active ​ ×b ​ , FLOPS 2N active ​ ​ ) ​ For 8×H100, single user: t bandwidth = 400 GB 26.8 TB/s ≈ 15 ms t bandwidth ​ = 26.8TB/s 400GB ​ ≈15ms t compute = 400 GFLOP 7.9 PFLOP/s ≈ 0.05 ms t compute ​ = 7.9PFLOP/s 400GFLOP ​ ≈0.05ms Bandwidth-bound: ~15ms/token ≈ 67 tokens/sec per user (consistent with Section 4) 6. MoE ROUTING MATHEMATICS (Missing: ~90%) The document says "a learned gating network routes tokens" but gives no formulas. Top-K Softmax Router g ( x ) = TopK ( Softmax ( W g ⋅ x + ϵ ) , k ) g(x)=TopK(Softmax(W g ​ ⋅x+ϵ),k) ​ where: x ∈ R d model x∈R d model ​ = token hidden state W g ∈ R E × d model W g ​ ∈R E×d model ​ = gating weight matrix ϵ ∼ N ( 0 , σ 2 ) ϵ∼N(0,σ 2 ) = optional noise for exploration k k = number of active experts (e.g. 2) Expert Output Combination y = ∑ i ∈ TopK g i ( x ) ⋅ E i ( x ) y= i∈TopK ∑ ​ g i ​ (x)⋅E i ​ (x) ​ where g i ( x ) g i ​ (x) is the normalized gate weight for expert i i, and E i ( x ) E i ​ (x) is expert i i's FFN output. Load Balancing Loss To prevent expert collapse (all tokens routing to few experts): L balance = α ⋅ E ⋅ ∑ i = 1 E f i ⋅ p i L balance ​ =α⋅E⋅ i=1 ∑ E ​ f i ​ ⋅p i ​ ​ where: f i = 1 T ∑ t = 1 T 1 [ i ∈ TopK ( g ( x t ) ) ] f i ​ = T 1 ​ ∑ t=1 T ​ 1[i∈TopK(g(x t ​ ))] = fraction of tokens dispatched to expert i i p i = 1 T ∑ t = 1 T g i ( x t ) p i ​ = T 1 ​ ∑ t=1 T ​ g i ​ (x t ​ ) = mean gate probability for expert i i α α = balancing coefficient (typically 0.01–0.1) Expert Capacity Factor C expert = CF × k × T E C expert ​ =CF× E k×T ​ ​ where CF (capacity factor) is typically 1.0–1.5. Tokens beyond capacity are dropped or rerouted. Router Z-Loss (stabilization) L z = 1 T ∑ t = 1 T ( log ⁡ ∑ i = 1 E e z i ( x t ) ) 2 L z ​ = T 1 ​ t=1 ∑ T ​ (log i=1 ∑ E ​ e z i ​ (x t ​ ) ) 2 ​ where z i = W g ⋅ x + b g z i ​ =W g ​ ⋅x+b g ​ (pre-softmax logits). This prevents logits from growing too large. 7. POSITIONAL ENCODING — RoPE AT 1M CONTEXT (Missing: 100%) Rotary Position Embedding (RoPE) f ( x m , m ) = x m ⋅ e i m θ j f(x m ​ ,m)=x m ​ ⋅e imθ j ​ ​ where θ j = base − 2 j / d θ j ​ =base −2j/d and j = 0 , 1 , … , d / 2 − 1 j=0,1,…,d/2−1. Standard base = 10,000. For 1M context, this must be extended. RoPE Scaling Methods NTK-Aware Scaling (YaRN): θ j ′ = ( base × α d / ( d − 2 ) ) − 2 j / d θ j ′ ​ =(base×α d/(d−2) ) −2j/d ​ where α = L target L train α= L train ​ L target ​ ​ (e.g. 1,000,000 8,192 ≈ 122 8,192 1,000,000 ​ ≈122). Linear Position Interpolation: f ( x m , m ) = x m ⋅ e i ( m / α ) θ j f(x m ​ ,m)=x m ​ ⋅e i(m/α)θ j ​ ​ Simply divides positions by scaling factor. Direct base increase (common for 1M): base ′ = 500,000 to 10,000,000 base ′ =500,000 to 10,000,000 Llama 3.1 uses base = 500,000 for 128K context. For 1M context, Opus 4.6 likely uses base ≈ 1,000,000–8,000,000 or a YaRN-style scheme. 8. TRAINING MEMORY (Activations, Optimizer States) (Missing: 100%) The document only covers weight memory. Training requires much more: Full Training Memory Per GPU (with ZeRO-3 / FSDP) M train = M weights + M optimizer + M gradients + M activations M train ​ =M weights ​ +M optimizer ​ +M gradients ​ +M activations ​ ​ With Adam optimizer (FP32 master weights): Component	Size per param	Total (2T params / distributed across 32K GPUs) FP16 weights	2 bytes	$2T \times 2 / 32K = $ 125 MB/GPU FP32 master weights	4 bytes	$2T \times 4 / 32K = $ 250 MB/GPU FP32 momentum (m)	4 bytes	250 MB/GPU FP32 variance (v)	4 bytes	250 MB/GPU FP16 gradients	2 bytes	125 MB/GPU Subtotal (params)	16 bytes/param	1.0 GB/GPU Activation memory (the dominant term): M act ≈ s × b × L ′ × d model × C a × bytes M act ​ ≈s×b×L ′ ×d model ​ ×C a ​ ×bytes ​ where s s = sequence length, b b = micro-batch size, L ′ L ′ = layers per pipeline stage, C a C a ​ ≈ 10–34 (depending on recomputation). With gradient checkpointing (recompute every L L ​ layers): M act ≈ s × b × L × d model × 34 × 2 M act ​ ≈s×b× L ​ ×d model ​ ×34×2 For s = 8192 s=8192, b = 1 b=1, L = 160 L=160, d = 16384 d=16384: M act ≈ 8192 × 1 × 13 × 16384 × 34 × 2 ≈ 119 GB M act ​ ≈8192×1×13×16384×34×2≈119 GB Total per GPU ≈ 1 GB (params) + 119 GB (activations) ≈ 120 GB — fits in H100 80GB only with aggressive pipeline/tensor parallelism and smaller micro-batches. 10. FINE-TUNING REQUIREMENTS — LoRA / QLoRA (Missing: 100%) LoRA Memory Formula M LoRA = M base_weights + r × ( d in + d out ) × L adapted × b M LoRA ​ =M base_weights ​ +r×(d in ​ +d out ​ )×L adapted ​ ×b ​ where r r = rank (typically 8–64), applied to L adapted L adapted ​ layers. For Opus 4.6 (hypothetical community fine-tune) Full model at INT4: ~1 TB loaded LoRA with r = 16 r=16 on attention Q/V matrices only (160 layers): LoRA params = 2 × 160 × 16 × ( 16384 + 16384 ) × 2 = 2 × 160 × 16 × 32768 × 2 LoRA params=2×160×16×(16384+16384)×2=2×160×16×32768×2 ≈ 336 M params ≈ 672 MB (FP16) ≈336M params≈672 MB (FP16) QLoRA memory: M QLoRA = N total 2 (4-bit base) + M LoRA (FP16) + M activations M QLoRA ​ = 2 N total ​ ​ (4-bit base)+M LoRA ​ (FP16)+M activations ​ ​ ≈ 1 TB (4-bit base) + 0.7 GB (LoRA) + ∼ 50 GB (activations) ≈ 1.05 TB total ≈1 TB (4-bit base)+0.7 GB (LoRA)+∼50 GB (activations)≈1.05 TB total Minimum hardware for QLoRA fine-tuning: 16× H100 80GB (1.28 TB total, enough for 4-bit base + LoRA + activations) 11. DISTRIBUTED PARALLELISM STRATEGIES (Missing: 100%) Four Types of Parallelism Used for Trillion-Param MoE text ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │          Parallelism for Opus 4.6            │ ├──────────────────────────────────────────────┤ │                                              │ │  1. Tensor Parallelism (TP)                  │ │     Split weight matrices across GPUs        │ │     within a node (fast NVLink)              │ │     Typical: TP=8 (one node)                 │ │                                              │ │  2. Expert Parallelism (EP)                  │ │     Different experts on different GPUs      │ │     128 experts / EP_degree = experts/GPU    │ │     Typical: EP=16–128                       │ │                                              │ │  3. Pipeline Parallelism (PP)               │ │     Different layers on different nodes      │ │     160 layers / PP = layers/stage           │ │     Typical: PP=8–16                         │ │                                              │ │  4. Data Parallelism (DP)                    │ │     Same model replicas, different data      │ │     DP = total_GPUs / (TP × PP × EP)        │ │                                              │ │  Total GPUs = TP × EP × PP × DP             │ │  e.g. 8 × 16 × 8 × 32 = 32,768 GPUs       │ │                                              │ └──────────────────────────────────────────────┘ Communication Volume (Expert Parallelism) All-to-All communication for MoE routing: V alltoall = 2 × b × s × d model × k E local × bytes V alltoall ​ =2×b×s×d model ​ × E local ​ k ​ ×bytes ​ Per step with b = 1 b=1, s = 8192 s=8192, d = 16384 d=16384, k = 2 k=2, E local = 8 E local ​ =8: V = 2 × 8192 × 16384 × 0.25 × 2 = 134 MB/step V=2×8192×16384×0.25×2=134 MB/step With $\sim$1,000 steps/second, this requires ~134 GB/s all-to-all bandwidth — feasible with InfiniBand NDR (400 Gbps per link). 12.
